Wife Says 'Akhtartu Ikhtiyarah' Results in Three Talaqs
Ruling on wife's emphatic response resulting in three talaqs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that when the wife says 'akhtartu ikhtiyarah' after being given the choice, three divorces occur by Hanafi consensus, and explain the role of emphasis in this ruling.

Core Idea
Saying 'akhtartu ikhtiyarah' emphasizes the choice, resulting in three talaqs by Hanafi consensus.

In the previous discussion, we saw that when a husband gives his wife the option of divorcing herself, various responses lead to different numbers of talaqs. This lesson focuses on a specific phrase: if the wife says 'akhtartu ikhtiyarah' (I chose a choosing), the Hanafi school unanimously rules that three divorces occur. Let's explore the reasoning behind this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's response
The wife says 'akhtartu ikhtiyarah' (I chose a choosing) after the husband says 'ikhtari' (choose).
The response contains the verbal noun 'ikhtiyarah'.
2
Understand the significance of 'ikhtiyarah'
'Ikhtiyarah' is a verbal noun (ism al-marrah) which literally indicates a single instance, but in this context it serves as emphasis (ta'kid) on the act of choosing.
The word 'ikhtiyarah' is used for emphasis.
3
Apply the principle of emphasis
Since a non-emphatic response (e.g., 'akhtartu nafsi') already results in multiple talaqs in certain cases, the emphatic form 'ikhtiyarah' makes the occurrence of three talaqs even more appropriate.
Emphasis strengthens the intention.
4
Note the consensus
All three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on this ruling.
No intra-madhhab disagreement.
5
State the result
The wife's statement results in three talaqs (divorces), ending the marriage irrevocably.
Three talaqs in one sitting constitute a major separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قالت اخترت اختيارة فهى ثلث في قولهم جميعا لانها للمرة فصارت كما اذا صرحت بها و لان الاختيارة للتاكيد و بدون التاكيد يقع الثلث فمع التاكيد اولى
And if she said: 'I chose a choosing' (akhtartu ikhtiyarah), then she is three (divorces) in the opinion of all (imams), because it is for multiple times (li al-marrah), so it becomes as if she explicitly stated it (the three divorces), and because the word 'ikhtiyarah' is for emphasis, and without emphasis three divorces occur, so with emphasis it is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This is a brief ruling stating that if the wife, after being given the option, says 'akhtartu ikhtiyarah' (I chose a choosing), then three divorces occur by consensus of all Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). The reason is that the word 'ikhtiyarah' is a verbal noun (ism al-marrah) that indicates a single instance, but in this context it emphasizes the act of choosing, and since without emphasis (when she says 'akhtartu nafsi') three divorces occur per the earlier dispute? Actually, careful: this follows the earlier dispute where when she says 'akhtartu nafsi' without repetition, one divorce occurs? No, the earlier ruling was: if she says 'ana akhtar nafsi', one talaq bain occurs. But here it says that without emphasis (i.e., just saying 'akhtartu nafsi') three divorces occur? That seems contradictory. Actually, the text says: 'and without emphasis three divorces occur' – but that refers to the case of repetition? Let's check the context. This passage comes after the discussion of repetition. Probably the text is saying: in the case where the husband repeats 'ikhtari' three times and the wife responds with something that indicates three choices, three divorces occur without the word 'ikhtiyarah'. So the emphasis ('ikhtiyarah') makes it even more certain. The ruling is that the wife's saying 'ikhtiyarah' results in three talaqs according to all. This is a straightforward memorization point.

Key Terms
اختيارة (ikhtiyarah): A verbal noun (masdar marrah) meaning 'a single choice'; used here for emphasis.
تاكيد (ta'kid): Emphasis. The word 'ikhtiyarah' serves as emphasis ensuring multiple divorces.

Key Takeaway
When the wife emphatically says 'akhtartu ikhtiyarah', three talaqs occur by Hanafi consensus due to the emphasis in her response.

Wife Says 'Qad Tallaqt Nafsi' Results in One Talaq Raj'i
Ruling when wife says 'I have divorced myself' or 'I choose myself with one divorce'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife uses the phrases 'qad tallaqtu nafsi' or 'akhtartu nafsi bi tatliqatin', and identify that the divorce is one revocable talaq (talaq raj'i).

Core Idea
When a wife says 'I have divorced myself' or 'I choose myself with one divorce', only one revocable talaq occurs.

This lesson covers a specific talaq scenario: the wife explicitly declares divorce upon herself. Even though she uses the word 'tallaqtu' (I have divorced), the Hanafi school holds that it results in a single revocable divorce, not an irrevocable one. The key distinction is that the wording implies separation only after the 'iddah, so the husband retains the right of return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's statement
The wife says either 'qad tallaqtu nafsi' or 'akhtartu nafsi bi tatliqatin'. Recognize these as the specific phrases triggering the ruling.
2
State the ruling
The outcome is one talaq raj'i (revocable divorce). The husband has the right to take her back during the 'iddah.
3
Understand the reasoning
The text explains that this wording necessitates separation only after the 'iddah expires. Thus, it is as if she chose herself after the 'iddah, meaning the husband can revoke during the 'iddah.
4
Contrast with other scenarios
Compare this to cases where the wife uses different wording or the husband pronounces divorce directly. The ruling is specific to these explicit phrases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولوقالت قد طلقت نفسی او اخترت نفسی بتطليقة فهي واحدة يملك الرجعة لان هذا اللفظ يوجب الانطلاق بعد انقضاء العدة فكانها اختارت نفسها بعد العدة
And if she said: 'I have divorced myself' (qad tallaqtu nafsi) or 'I choose myself with one divorce' (akhtartu nafsi bi tatliqatin), then it is one (divorce) and he (the husband) owns the right of return (raj'ah), because this wording necessitates the separation after the expiration of the 'iddah, so it is as if she chose herself after the 'iddah.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This ruling addresses a different scenario: the wife explicitly uses the word 'divorce' (tallaqtu) or says 'I choose myself with one divorce'. In this case, only one revocable talaq (talaq raj'i) occurs. The reason provided is that the wording 'qad tallaqtu nafsi' implies that the divorce is effective from the utterance, but the husband retains the right to take her back during the 'iddah (waiting period). Similarly, if she says 'akhtartu nafsi bi tatliqatin' (I choose myself with one divorce), it is understood as a single, revocable divorce. The text explains that this wording necessitates separation only after the 'iddah expires, meaning it is not immediately irrevocable. Thus, it is as if she chose herself after the 'iddah, i.e., the divorce becomes effective immediately but the husband can revoke it. This is a clear ruling to be memorized.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The husband's right to take back his wife during the 'iddah after a revocable divorce.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce during which the husband may revoke.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Talaq bain
B) Talaq raj'i
C) Three talaqs
D) No talaq

Key Takeaway
When the wife says 'qad tallaqtu nafsi' or 'akhtartu nafsi bi tatliqatin', the result is one talaq raj'i, preserving the husband's right of return during the iddah.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Wife's Ordered Response in Tafwid al-Talaq
Intra-Hanafi dispute on talaq wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the number of divorces when a wife responds with ordered terms 'first, middle, last' after being given 'ikhtari' three times, and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
When three divorces are owned as a set, ordering is futile—only the choice matters.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal effect of a wife's response when given the option to choose divorce. The husband says 'ikhtari' three times, and the wife responds with 'I chose the first, the middle, and the last.' Does her ordering matter? Two opinions emerge from the school's founders: Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and issue
The husband repeats 'ikhtari' three times, delegating the choice of divorce. The wife responds with ordered terms: first, middle, last. The issue is how many divorces occur.
The repetition of 'ikhtari' itself indicates a threefold choice.
2
State the two positions
Imam Abu Hanifa holds three divorces; the Sahibayn hold one divorce. Both agree the husband's intention is not needed.
3
Follow the reasoning of each
Abu Hanifa argues the ordering is futile because three divorces in ownership cannot be ordered. The Sahibayn argue the words 'first', 'middle', 'last' refer to individual acts, even if ordering is irrelevant, so only the first is chosen.
Analogy: people in a house have no order.
4
Identify the practical difference
Three divorces are ba'in (irrevocable), ending marriage immediately. One divorce is revocable, allowing reconciliation during the waiting period.
This changes the marital status drastically.
5
Determine the relied-upon view
The Hidayah commentary implies Imam Abu Hanifa's view (three divorces) is the mufta bihi, but both are recorded.

Translation: And if he said to the woman: 'Choose, choose, choose.' The woman said: 'I chose the first, the middle, and the last.' Then according to Imam Sahib's statement, three divorces will occur. And the husband's intention is not needed. Because the repetition indicates divorce, as repeated 'ikhtiyar' is about divorce. The proof of the two companions (Sahibayn) is that the mention of 'first' and what stands in its place, even if not useful in terms of order, is useful in terms of being singular. So it is considered in what is useful. And Imam Sahib's proof is that this description is futile. Because (the three divorces) that are combined in ownership have no order. Like people gathered in a place (have no order). And speech is for order. And individuals are among its necessities. So when it became futile with respect to the origin, it also became futile with respect to the derivative. And if the woman said 'I chose, I chose it (ikhtartu ikhtiyaraha),' by consensus they will be three. Because the word 'ikhtiyaraha' is for one time. So it is as if she specified the word 'once'. And because 'ikhtiyaraha' is for emphasis, and without emphasis three occur, so with emphasis all the more three occur. And if the woman said 'I have made divorce occur upon myself' or 'I have chosen myself with one divorce,' then it is one, and he owns the right of return. Because these words necessitate the occurrence of divorce after the passing of the waiting period. So it is as if she chose herself after the waiting period. Explanation: If a man said to his wife the word 'ikhtari' three times, and his wife replied 'I chose the first, the middle, and the last,' then according to Imam Abu Hanifa three divorces occur. And the husband does not need intention nor the mention of the word 'self'. And the two companions say that one divorce will occur. And the husband's intention is also not needed. The proof that the husband does not need intention is that the repetition of the word 'ikhtiyar' indicates the meaning of divorce. Because repeated 'ikhtiyar' is about divorce. So when there is a context for the meaning of divorce, the husband's intention is not needed. The proof of the two companions is that 'first', 'middle', and 'last' are names of ordered individual parts. Because 'first' is the feminine of 'first', and 'first' is the name of the preceding part, and 'middle' is the feminine of 'middle', and 'middle' is the name of that part which is preceded by as many as it is succeeded, and 'last' is the name of the succeeding part. And this place is not a context for order. Therefore, the ordering becomes futile, and the individuals remain. That is, even if this speech is not useful for order, it is useful for individuals. So what is useful will be considered. As if the woman said 'I chose the first divorce.' Because her saying 'I chose the first' means I chose what was entrusted to me by the word 'first', and what is entrusted by the word 'first' is one divorce. For this reason, one divorce will occur. Imam Sahib's proof is that this description, i.e., 'first', 'middle', 'last', is futile. Because the three divorces that are combined in the husband's ownership have no order. Like many people gathered in a house, they have no order. And the rule is that everything which has no order, speech that is for order becomes futile. And here 'first', 'middle', 'last' are for order. So when the word became futile in terms of order, it also became futile in terms of individuals. Because order is the origin and individuals are among its necessities. So when it became futile with respect to the origin, it also became futile with respect to what depends on it. And when the word became futile in both aspects, the woman's statement 'I chose' remains.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a specific wording in delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq). The husband says 'ikhtari' (choose) three times to his wife. The wife responds by saying 'I chose the first, the middle, and the last' (اخترت الأولى والوسطى والآخرة). The key issue is whether the wife's ordering of her response has any legal effect. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that three divorces occur, regardless of the ordering, because the three choices are all in the husband's ownership and cannot be ordered as first, middle, last. The description of order is futile, and the wife's mere statement 'I chose' suffices to effect three divorces. The Sahibayn argue that the words 'first', 'middle', 'last' are meaningful in that they refer to individual acts of choosing, even if the ordering is irrelevant. Thus, the wife's response effectively selects only one divorce (the first one), so only one divorce occurs. Both sides agree that the husband's intention is not necessary because the repetition of 'ikhtiyar' and the context clearly indicate divorce. The passage also briefly mentions two other scenarios: (1) if the wife says 'اخترت اختیارہ' (I chose, I choose it), by consensus three divorces occur because it emphasizes the choice, and (2) if the wife says 'طلقت نفسی' (I divorced myself) or 'اخترت نفسی بتطلیقہ' (I chose myself with one divorce), then one revocable divorce occurs. The commentary is detailed, providing the reasoning of each imam. The author of Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the proofs and uses analogies (e.g., people in a house) to clarify Imam Abu Hanifa's rationale.

Key Terms
اختیار (ikhtiyar): Choice or option given to the wife by the husband to divorce herself.
اولی (ula): Feminine of 'first'; here it designates the first of three ordered options.
وسطی (wusta): Feminine of 'middle'; designates the middle option.
اخیرہ (akhira): Feminine of 'last'; designates the final option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'ikhtari' three times and the wife responds 'اخترت الأولى والوسطى والآخرة', how many divorces occur according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) Three divorces
B) One divorce
C) No divorce
D) Two divorces

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute on a wife's ordered response, the ruling depends on whether ordering is deemed futile—Abu Hanifa's view (three divorces) is relied upon.

Delegated Divorce: Explicit Words by Wife – Revocable or Irrevocable?
Ruling with proof and critical refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the corrected Hanafi ruling when a wife uses explicit divorce wording after being delegated the right to divorce with the word 'ikhtari', and explain why the initial text's mention of revocability is a scribal error.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, explicit words by the wife yield ba'in divorce, not raj'i — the text's 'raj'i' is a scribal error.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a third scenario in delegated divorce. The matn initially rules that when the wife uses explicit words like 'tallaqt nafsi', the divorce is revocable. However, the commentators of Hidayah identify a scribal error: the correct view, supported by Zahir al-Riwayah texts, is that such a divorce is irrevocable. Let's analyse why the delegation mechanism changes the nature of the divorce, and why the original text needed correction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
The husband says 'ikhtari' three times to his wife, delegating the choice to divorce. The wife responds with 'tallaqt nafsi' or 'ikhtartu nafsi bi-tatliqa'. According to the matn of Hidayah, one revocable divorce occurs and the husband retains the right to take her back.
2
Analyse the proof given in the matn
The proof: Words like 'tallaqt' and 'tatliqa' are explicit (sarih) terms. In normal divorce they cause revocability at the moment of utterance, but become irrevocable after the waiting period. Thus the matn holds that this scenario yields a revocable divorce.
The proof conflates the context of normal divorce with delegated divorce.
3
Understand the objection and response
An objection is raised: The delegation uses 'ikhtari' which is a kinayah (allusive) word; kinayah gives ba'in divorce, not raj'i. The response: The woman effectively chooses herself after the waiting period, but this does not address the core issue of delegation type.
The response in the matn is weak, leading to the correction.
4
Explain the correction by the commentators
The commentators of Hidayah state that the phrase 'يملك الرجعة' (he owns the right of return) is a scribal error. The correct view: because the delegation was with 'ikhtari' (a kinayah word), the wife can only effect a ba'in divorce. Thus one divorce occurs, and the husband does not have the right to return.
This correction is based on the authoritative Zahir al-Riwayah books.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce, no right of return. The scribal error is documented, but the correct ruling is clear from the core texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تیسری صورت یہ ہے کہ اگر عورت نے تین بار اختاری کہنے کے جواب میں طلقت نفسی یا اخترت نفسی بتطلیقہ کہا تو ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی ۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ لفظ عدت گزرنے کے بعد موجب الطلاق یعنی بینونت کا موجب ہے۔ کیونکہ لفظ طلقت اور تطلیقۃ الفاظ صریح میں سے ہیں اور الفاظ صریح انقضاء عدت کے بعد بینونت کا موجب اور وقوع کے وقت طلاق رجعی کا موجب ہیں۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ اس صورت میں طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ فكانها اختارت سے اعتراض کا جواب ہے۔ اعتراض یہ ہے کہ اس وقت حکم تفویض طلاق کے مطابق نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عورت کی طرف اختیار سپر د کیا گیا ہے۔ اور لفظ اختار بنونت کا فائدہ دیتا ہے نہ کہ رجعت کا۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ عورت نے گویا اپنے آپ کو عدت کے بعد اختیار کیا۔ اور عدت کے بعد بینونت ثابت ہو جاتی ہے۔ پس حکم طلاق تفویض کے مطابق ہو گیا۔ شارحین ہدایہ نے اس موقع پر فرمایا کہ یملک الرجعة عبارت میں غلطی ہے ۔ کاتب سے نسیان ہوا ہے۔ کیونکہ عورت نے بحکم تفویض تصرف کیا ہے۔ اور تفویض طلاق بائن کے ساتھ ہوتی ہے۔ اسلئے کہ لفظ اختیار کے ساتھ سپرد کرنا الفاظ کنایہ میں سے ہے اور کنایات سے طلاق بائن واقع ہوتی ہے۔ پس عورت اپنے اوپر صرف طلاق بائن واقع کر سکتی ہے۔ صحیح روایت ہے۔ فهي واحدة ولا يملك الرجعة۔ کیونکہ مبسوط، جامع کبیر، زیادات اور جامع صغیر کے عام نسخوں میں اسی طرح ہے۔
Third scenario: If the woman, in response to the husband saying 'ikhtari' three times, said 'I divorced myself' or 'I chose myself with one divorce,' then one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) will occur. The proof is that these words are a cause of divorce after the passing of the waiting period, i.e., a cause of irrevocability (baynunah). Because the words 'tallaqt' and 'tatliqa' are among explicit words, and explicit words cause irrevocability after the end of the waiting period and cause revocable divorce at the time of occurrence. So it is established that in this case a revocable divorce occurs. This is the answer to the objection: '**It would be as if she chose after the waiting period.**' The objection is that at that time the ruling would not conform to delegated divorce, because the choice was entrusted to the woman, and the word 'ikhtiyar' gives irrevocability, not revocability. The answer is that the woman effectively chose herself after the waiting period, and after the waiting period irrevocability is established, so the ruling conforms to delegated divorce. The commentators of Hidayah said at this point that the phrase 'he owns the right of return' (يملك الرجعة) is a mistake; the copyist made an error. Because the woman acted by virtue of delegation, and delegated divorce occurs as an irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in). This is because entrusting with the word 'ikhtiyar' is among kinayah (allusive) words, and from kinayah an irrevocable divorce occurs. So the woman can only effect an irrevocable divorce upon herself. The correct narration is: 'It is one, and he does not own the right of return.' Because in the common copies of al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat, and al-Jami' al-Saghir it is like this.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage discusses a third scenario: when the wife, after the husband's three 'ikhtari', uses explicit divorce wording like 'tallaqt nafsi' (I divorced myself) or 'ikhtartu nafsi bi-tatliqa' (I chose myself with one divorce). The initial ruling stated in the matn of Hidayah is that this results in one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i), with the husband retaining the right to take her back during the waiting period. The proof is that explicit words (صریح) like 'tallaqt' cause revocability at the time of occurrence but become irrevocable after the waiting period. However, the commentator (Sharhin al-Hidayah) points out an error in the text: the phrase 'يملك الرجعة' (he owns the right of return) is incorrect. The correct view is that since the delegation of divorce was through the word 'ikhtiyar', which is a kinayah (allusive) expression, the resulting divorce is ba'in (irrevocable). Therefore, the wife can only effect a ba'in divorce, and the husband does not have the right to take her back. The commentary cites the authoritative sources (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat, al-Jami' al-Saghir) to support the correct narration. This lesson illustrates the importance of textual criticism in fiqh and the hierarchy of sources: Zahir al-Riwayah books override later scribal errors.

Key Terms
طلاق رجعی (talaq raj'i): Revocable divorce where husband can take wife back without new marriage.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; husband cannot take wife back without new marriage.
کنایہ (kinayah): Allusive words that imply divorce but are not explicit; require intention or context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the corrected view of the commentators of Hidayah, when the wife says 'طلقت نفسی' in response to delegated 'ikhtari', what type of divorce occurs?
A) One revocable divorce with right of return
B) One irrevocable divorce without right of return
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
When a husband delegates divorce using a kinayah word like 'ikhtari', the wife's explicit divorce words result in an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce, not revocable – a scribal error in the original text is corrected by Zahir al-Riwayah sources.

Husband Says 'امرک بیدک فی تطلیقہ' – One Revocable Divorce
Ruling on delegation with 'tatliqa' specified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband delegates divorce limited to one tatliqa and identify why it is revocable.

Core Idea
Delegating divorce with 'tatliqa' limits the wife's choice to one revocable divorce.

In the previous lesson, we saw that unrestricted delegation (e.g., 'امرک بیدک') could result in three divorces. Here, the husband adds the word 'في تطليقة' (in one divorce), explicitly restricting the delegation. This restriction changes the outcome: a single revocable divorce, with the husband retaining the right to take back his wife during the waiting period. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation formula
The husband said 'امرک بیدک فی تطلیقہ' or 'اختاری تطلیقہ'. Note the restriction to one divorce.
2
State the ruling
The wife choosing herself results in one divorce (واحدة) and the husband owns the right of return (يملك الرجعة).
3
Analyze the proof
The text says 'لانه جعل لها الاختيار لكن بتطليقة وهي معقبة للرجعة'. The word 'تطليقة' specifies a single revocable divorce. In Hanafi law, explicit words of divorce (sarih) produce a revocable divorce unless otherwise indicated.
4
Contrast with unrestricted delegation
Without the restriction, the wife's choice could effect three divorces. This highlights the importance of wording.

Source Text

وان قال لها امرك بيدك فى تطليقة او اختارى تطليقة فاختارت نفسها فهي واحدة يملك الرجعة لانه جعل لها الاختيار لكن بتطليقة وهي معقبة للرجعة
ترجمہ اگر ( مرد نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا طلاق دینے کے بارے میں تیرا معاملہ تیرے ہاتھ میں ہے۔ یا تو ۔ طلاق کو اختیار کر۔ پھر اس نے اپنے نفس کو اختیار کر لیا تو ایک ہے، رجعت کا بالک ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس نے عورت کو اختیار دیا لیکن تطلیقہ کیساتھ اور تطلیقہ کے بعد رجعت ہوتی ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ مرد نے اپنی منکوحہ کو امرک بیدک فی تطلیقۃ یا اختیار تطلیقة کے ساتھ اختیار دیا۔عورت نے اختیارکرلیا تو طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ کیونکہ عورت کو لفظ تطلیقہ کے ساتھ اختیار دیا گیا ہے۔ اور لفظ تطلیقہ سے اگر طلاق دی جائے تو طلاق رجعی واقع
And if he said to her: 'Your affair is in your hand in one divorce' or 'Choose one divorce,' and she chose herself, then it is one, and he owns the right of return, because he gave her the choice but with one divorce, and one divorce is followed by return. The woman chose, so a revocable divorce occurs, because the woman was given choice with the word tatliqa, and if divorce is given with tatliqa, a revocable divorce occurs.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn of Hidayah: When a husband delegates divorce to his wife but limits it to a single divorce by using the word 'tatliqa' (one divorce), and the wife chooses herself, the result is one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i), with the husband retaining the right to take her back during the waiting period. The proof is that 'tatliqa' specifies a single revocable divorce. The commentary explains that the key distinction from the previous cases is that the delegation is explicitly restricted to one divorce, so the wife's choice cannot exceed that. This contrasts with the earlier cases where the delegation was unrestricted (three 'ikhtari') and the wife's response could effect three divorces. The ruling is straightforward and follows the general rule that explicit words of divorce (sarih) produce a revocable divorce.

Correcting a Scribal Error: Tafwīḍ with Kināyah Yields Bā'in Divorce
Critical refinement correcting a scribal error in the Hidayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the phrase 'yamlik al-raj'ah' is incorrect in the context of tafwīḍ with kināyah and identify the correct ruling that the husband has no right of return.

Core Idea
Tafwīḍ with allusive words (kināyah) yields a bā'in divorce — the husband has no right of return (raj'ah).

This passage reveals a crucial textual correction in the Hidayah. Commentators point out that a scribal error led to a statement that the husband retains the right to take back his wife after a delegated divorce. We'll examine why this is impossible when the delegation uses allusive words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual claim
The original (erroneous) text stated 'yamlik al-raj'ah' — meaning the husband has the right to take back his wife during iddah.
2
Understand why it's an error
Because the wife acted by tafwīḍ using the word 'ikhtiyār' (choice), which is kināyah. In Hanafi law, kināyah yields a bā'in (irrevocable) divorce, which extinguishes the husband's right to return.
Bā'in divorce means the marriage ends immediately; only a new marriage can reunite them.
3
Confirm the correct ruling
The correct text is 'it is one (i.e., a single bā'in divorce) and he does not have the right of return.' This is attested in the core sources: al-Mabsūt, al-Jāmi' al-Kabīr, al-Ziyādāt, and common copies of al-Jāmi' al-Ṣaghīr.
4
Apply to practice
When a husband delegates divorce using an allusive word (e.g., 'Choose yourself'), the wife can only effect a bā'in divorce. The husband cannot take her back during iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شارحین ہدایہ نے اس موقع پر فرمایا کہ یملک الرجعة عبارت میں غلطی ہے ۔ کاتب سے نسیان ہوا ہے۔ کیونکہ عورت نے بحکم تفویض تصرف کیا ہے۔ اور تفویض طلاق بائن کے ساتھ ہوتی ہے۔ اسلئے کہ لفظ اختیار کے ساتھ سپرد کرنا الفاظ کنایہ میں سے ہے اور کنایات سے طلاق بائن واقع ہوتی ہے۔ پس عورت اپنے اوپر صرف طلاق بائن واقع کر سکتی ہے۔ صحیح روایت ہے۔ فهي واحدة ولا يملك الرجعة۔ کیونکہ مبسوط، جامع کبیر، زیادات اور جامع صغیر کے عام نسخوں میں اسی طرح ہے۔
The commentators of Hidayah said at this point that the phrase 'he has the right of return' (yamlik al-raj'ah) is a mistake. It is a scribal error (nasayān). Because the woman acted by virtue of delegation (tafwīḍ). And delegation of divorce occurs with irrevocable divorce. Because delegating with the word 'ikhtiyār' is among allusive words (kināyah), and from allusive words an irrevocable divorce (bā'in) occurs. So the woman can only effect an irrevocable divorce upon herself. The correct narration is 'it is one and he does not have the right of return.' Because in al-Mabsūṭ, al-Jāmi' al-Kabīr, al-Ziyādāt, and the common copies of al-Jāmi' al-Ṣaghīr, it is thus.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This is a critical refinement by later commentators (shāriḥīn) of al-Hidayah, correcting a textual error in the manuscript. The original text apparently stated 'yamlik al-raj'ah' (he has the right to take her back). The commentators argue this is a scribal mistake (nasayān), because the woman acted by virtue of tafwīḍ (delegation). In Hanafi law, when the husband delegates divorce using an allusive word (kināyah) like 'ikhtiyār' (choice), the divorce effected is irrevocable (bā'in), not revocable (raj'ī). Therefore, the wife can only effect a bā'in divorce upon herself, and the husband has no right of return (raj'ah). They cite the authority of the core books of the school (al-Mabsūṭ, al-Jāmi' al-Kabīr, al-Ziyādāt, and common copies of al-Jāmi' al-Ṣaghīr) to confirm that the correct text reads 'it is one and he does not have the right of return.' This passage demonstrates the importance of textual criticism in preserving the correct rulings of the madhhab.

Key Terms
تفویض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of divorce by the husband to the wife, giving her authority to divorce herself.
کنایہ (kināyah): Allusive words that imply divorce but are not explicit; they require intention (niyyah).
یملک الرجعہ (yamlik al-raj'ah): He has the right to return (take back the wife during 'iddah). The passage argues this is a scribal error.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No divorce at all

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband delegates divorce using allusive words (kināyah), the resulting divorce is bā'in, and the husband has no right to return (raj'ah).

Three Scenarios of Talaq by Delegation: Wife's Response to 'Akhtari'
Ruling with proof for three cases of talaq by delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the three scenarios of the wife's response to the husband's repeated 'akhtari' and apply the linguistic reasoning (ta'kīd) and the distinction between explicit and delegated words to determine the type and number of divorces.

Core Idea
The wife's response determines whether three divorces or one revocable divorce occurs—emphasis confirms three, explicit words limit to one.

When a husband delegates divorce by saying 'akhtari' three times, the wife's choice of words determines the number and type of divorce. This lesson unpacks three possible responses and the Hanafi reasoning behind each ruling, based on linguistic analysis and qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's delegation
The husband says 'akhtari' (choose) three times. In Hanafi fiqh, this repetition is considered a delegation of three divorces, not one. Understand that the number of divorces is determined by the husband's delegation.
If the husband had said 'akhtari' only once, the delegation would be for one divorce. The repetition changes the scope.
2
First scenario: wife says 'akhtartu'
The wife responds with 'akhtartu' (I chose) without any emphasis or additional wording. According to the Hanafi school, three divorces occur immediately because the wife's response matches the delegated three.
The first scenario is implied from the text: 'And if the woman says 'I chose' in response to the husband's saying 'choose' three times, then three divorces occur.'
3
Second scenario: wife says 'akhtartu akhtārahā'
The wife uses the emphatic form 'akhtārahā'. By consensus, three divorces occur. The reasoning is that 'akhtārahā' is for emphasis (ta'kīd). Since without emphasis three divorces occur, with emphasis they occur a fortiori.
The proof is based on qiyas: the emphatic form reinforces the action, so the number of divorces is not reduced.
4
Third scenario: wife says 'tallaqtu nafsī' or 'akhtartu nafsī bi-tatliqah'
The wife uses explicit words for divorce: 'tallaqtu nafsī' (I divorced myself) or 'akhtartu nafsī bi-tatliqah' (I chose myself with one pronouncement). In this case, only one revocable divorce (talāq raj'ī) occurs. The words 'tallaqtu' and 'tatlīqatan' are explicit (ṣarīḥ) and indicate a single pronouncement.
Explicit words cause revocable divorce at the moment of utterance but become irrevocable after the 'iddah. This does not contradict delegation because the wife effectively chooses separation after the 'iddah.
5
Analyze the objection and its response
An objection arises: if delegation gives the wife authority to choose, and 'ikhtār' (choice) leads to baynūnah (irrevocable separation), how can the third scenario produce a revocable divorce? The answer is that the wife's choice is considered to take effect after the 'iddah, where baynūnah is established. Thus, the ruling conforms to delegation.
This demonstrates the Hanafi method of harmonizing textual interpretation with the underlying legal principle of delegation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور اگر مرد کے قول تین مرتبہ اختیاری کے جواب میں یہ عورت اخترت کہہ دیتی تو تین طلاقیں واقع ہوتیں پس ایسے ہی یہاں بھی تین واقع ہوں گی۔
دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ اگر عورت نے تین بار اختیاری کے جواب میں اخترت اختیارہ کہ دیا تو بالاتفاق تین طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ اختیارہ کی تا مرہ کیلئے ہے۔ پس یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ یوں کہا اخترت نفسی مرہ یعنی میں نے ایک ہی مرتبہ اختیار کر لیا اور اس کلام سے تین طلاقیں واقع ہو جاتی ہیں۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ لفظ اختیارہ تاکید کیلئے ہے۔ اور بغیر تاکید کے تین واقع ہو جائیں تاکید کے ساتھ بدرجہ اولی تین واقع ہوں گی۔
تیسری صورت یہ ہے کہ اگر عورت نے تین بار اختاری کہنے کے جواب میں طلقت نفسی یا اخترت نفسی بتطلیقہ کہا تو ایک طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی ۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ لفظ عدت گزرنے کے بعد موجب الطلاق یعنی بینونت کا موجب ہے۔ کیونکہ لفظ طلقت اور تطلیقۃ الفاظ صریح میں سے ہیں اور الفاظ صریح انقضاء عدت کے بعد بینونت کا موجب اور وقوع کے وقت طلاق رجعی کا موجب ہیں۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ اس صورت میں طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔
فكانها اختارت سے اعتراض کا جواب ہے۔ اعتراض یہ ہے کہ اس وقت حکم تفویض طلاق کے مطابق نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عورت کی طرف اختیار سپر د کیا گیا ہے۔ اور لفظ اختار بنونت کا فائدہ دیتا ہے نہ کہ رجعت کا۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ عورت نے گویا اپنے آپ کو عدت کے بعد اختیار کیا۔ اور عدت کے بعد بینونت ثابت ہو جاتی ہے۔ پس حکم طلاق تفویض کے مطابق ہو گیا۔
And if the woman says 'I chose' in response to the husband's saying 'choose' three times, then three divorces occur. So similarly here also three occur. The second case: if the woman, in response to three times 'choose', says 'I chose an emphatic choosing', then by consensus three divorces occur. The proof is that 'akhthār' is for emphasis. So it becomes as if she said 'I chose myself once', and from that statement three divorces occur. The second proof is that the word 'akhthār' is for emphasis, and without emphasis three occur, so with emphasis a fortiori three occur. The third case: if the woman, in response to three times 'choose', says 'I divorced myself' or 'I chose myself with a single pronouncement', then one revocable divorce occurs. The proof is that this word after the passage of 'iddah causes separation (baynūnah). Because the words 'tallaqtu' and 'tatlīqatan' are among explicit words, and explicit words after the 'iddah cause separation, and at the time of occurrence cause revocable divorce. So it is established that in this case a revocable divorce occurs. This is the answer to the objection from 'kaannahā ikhtārat'. The objection is that at that time the ruling would not conform to delegation of divorce, because authority was delegated to the woman, and the word 'ikhtār' gives baynūnah, not revocability. The answer is that the woman effectively chose herself after the 'iddah, and after the 'iddah separation is established. So the ruling conforms to delegation.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing three scenarios of talaq by delegation (tafwīḍ al-ṭalāq) when the husband repeats 'akhtarī' (choose) three times. In the first scenario, the wife responds 'akhtartu' (I chose) – three divorces occur because the husband's repetition is considered a delegation of three. The second scenario: the wife says 'akhtartu akhtārahā' (I chose emphatically) – by consensus three divorces occur, because 'akhtārahā' is the emphatic form (ta'kīd), and since without emphasis three occur, with emphasis they a fortiori occur. The third scenario: the wife says 'tallaqtu nafsī' (I divorced myself) or 'akhtartu nafsī bi-tatliqah' (I chose myself with one pronouncement) – one revocable (raj'ī) divorce occurs. The reasoning is that 'tallaqtu' and 'tatlīqatan' are explicit words (ṣarīḥ) which, after the 'iddah, cause irrevocable separation (baynūnah), but at the time of utterance they cause revocable divorce. The passage then addresses an objection based on the principle 'kaannahā ikhtārat' (as if she chose after 'iddah). The objection is that this ruling contradicts delegation because the wife chooses baynūnah, not revocability. The answer is that the wife's choice is effective after the 'iddah, which indeed establishes baynūnah, so it conforms to delegation. This is a Hanafi ruling from the Hidayah, based on linguistic analysis and qiyas.

Key Terms
اختیاری (ikhtiyārī): The husband's instruction to the wife to choose divorce, repeated three times.
اختیارہ (ikhtārahā): An emphatic form of 'akhtartu' meaning 'I chose emphatically', used in the second scenario.
تطلیقہ (taṭlīqatan): The verbal noun of divorce, used as an explicit word (ṣarīḥ) indicating one pronouncement.

Delegated Divorce Limited to One Pronouncement
Ruling with proof for delegation limited to one pronouncement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband delegates divorce limited to one pronouncement (tatliqah), explain the reasoning based on the word 'tatliqah', and distinguish it from a general delegation.

Core Idea
Delegation limited to 'tatliqah' yields one revocable divorce with right of return.

This lesson examines a specific delegation formula: when the husband says 'Your affair is in your hand concerning one pronouncement' or 'Choose one pronouncement.' The wife chooses herself. What is the legal effect? The Hidayah clarifies that the word 'tatliqah' restricts the delegation to one revocable pronouncement, preserving the husband's right of return (raj'ah). We will walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Formula and the Act
The husband uses either 'amruk biyadik fi tatliqah' or 'akhtari tatliqah'. The wife exercises her choice by choosing herself. This is a form of tafwīḍ al-talāq (delegated divorce).
The delegation is explicitly limited to one pronouncement.
2
Determine the Ruling from the Text
The text states: 'it is one and he has the right of return.' So the ruling is one revocable divorce (talaq raj'ī).
Revocable means the husband can take her back during the 'iddah without a new marriage.
3
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The proof is the word 'tatliqah' itself. The author says: 'Because he gave her the choice, but with tatliqah, and tatliqah is followed by raj'ah.'
The word 'tatliqah' is an explicit term (ṣarīḥ) for one pronouncement of divorce.
4
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah (How the Proof Yields the Ruling)
In Hanafi fiqh, explicit words of divorce (ṣarīḥ) like 'anti taliq' result in revocable divorce unless specified otherwise. Here, 'tatliqah' is even more explicit because it limits to one pronouncement. So the divorce is revocable.
If the delegation had been general (e.g. 'amruk biyadik' without specifying), the wife could choose three divorces, which would be bā'in. But 'tatliqah' restricts the choice.
5
Note Cross-Madhhab Ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g. Shafi'i) may consider any delegated divorce as bā'in if the wife chooses herself, because they view delegation as a form of agency giving the wife full control. Hanafis, however, look at the wording used by the husband.
Practical difference: Under Hanafi, husband can revoke; under Shafi'i view, divorce becomes irrevocable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال لها امرك بيدك فى تطليقة او اختارى تطليقة فاختارت نفسها فهي واحدة يملك الرجعة لانه جعل لها الاختيار لكن بتطليقة وهي معقبة للرجعة
ترجمہ اگر ( مرد نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا طلاق دینے کے بارے میں تیرا معاملہ تیرے ہاتھ میں ہے۔ یا تو ۔ طلاق کو اختیار کر۔ پھر اس نے اپنے نفس کو اختیار کر لیا تو ایک ہے، رجعت کا بالک ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس نے عورت کو اختیار دیا لیکن تطلیقہ کیساتھ اور تطلیقہ کے بعد رجعت ہوتی ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ مرد نے اپنی منکوحہ کو امرک بیدک فی تطلیقۃ یا اختیار تطلیقة کے ساتھ اختیار دیا۔عورت نے اختیارکرلیا تو طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ کیونکہ عورت کو لفظ تطلیقہ کے ساتھ اختیار دیا گیا ہے۔ اور لفظ تطلیقہ سے اگر طلاق دی جائے تو طلاق رجعی واقع
And if he said to her 'Your affair is in your hand concerning one pronouncement' or 'Choose one pronouncement', and she chose herself, then it is one and he has the right of return (raj'ah). Because he gave her the choice, but with 'tatliqah', and 'tatliqah' is followed by raj'ah. Translation: If (the husband said to his wife 'Your affair is in your hand concerning divorce' or 'Choose divorce.' Then she chose herself, then it is one, he will have the right of return. Because he gave her choice but with 'tatliqah', and after 'tatliqah' raj'ah occurs. Explanation: The husband gave his wife choice with the words 'amruk biyadik fi tatliqah' or 'akhtari tatliqah'. The wife chose, so a revocable divorce occurs. Because the wife was given choice with the word 'tatliqah', and if divorce is given with the word 'tatliqah', a revocable divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof from the Hidayah. The husband says either 'amruk biyadik fi tatliqah' (your affair is in your hand concerning one pronouncement) or 'akhtari tatliqah' (choose one pronouncement). The wife chooses herself. The ruling is: one revocable divorce occurs, and the husband has the right of return (raj'ah). The reasoning is that the husband restricted the delegation to 'tatliqah', which is an explicit word (ṣarīḥ) for one pronouncement. In Hanafi law, divorce with explicit words in the mode of 'tatliqah' results in revocable divorce, not bā'in. Therefore, because the husband specified 'tatliqah', the wife's choice also results in revocable divorce. The Urdu commentary clarifies that the wife was given authority with the word 'tatliqah', and that word yields revocable divorce.

Key Terms
امرک بیدک فی تطلیقہ (amruk biyadik fī ṭalqah): Your affair is in your hand concerning one pronouncement – a formula delegating one revocable divorce.
تطلیقہ (ṭalqah (or taṭlīqah)): One pronouncement of divorce, considered explicit (ṣarīḥ) and resulting in revocable divorce.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
When a husband delegates divorce limited to one pronouncement (tatliqah), the resulting divorce is one revocable (raj'ī) with right of return.

Effect of Wife's 'With One' When Husband Intends Three in Tafwid
Ruling with proof for effect of wife's response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband says 'amruk biyadik' intending three divorces and the wife responds 'with one', and explain the proof via qiyas and linguistic analysis.

Core Idea
The husband's intention of three overrides the wife's mention of 'one' in delegated divorce.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in talaq tafwid (delegated divorce). The husband says 'amruk biyadik' (your affair is in your hand) with the intention of three divorces. The wife responds 'akhtartu nafsi bi-wahidah' (I chose myself with one). A student might think only one divorce occurs. However, the Hanafi school rules three divorces. We will uncover the legal reasoning: the nature of amr bil-yad as tamlik (grant of ownership) and the linguistic construction that treats 'with one' as an adjective describing a single act of choice, not limiting the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the husband's action? He says 'amruk biyadik' intending three. What is the wife's response? She says 'with one'. The ruling: three divorces occur (sahih).
The ruling is applicable only when the husband intends three.
2
Locate the dalil
The author gives two proofs: (1) 'ikhtiyar' is a valid response to 'amr bil-yad' because both are tamlik like takhyir. (2) 'wahidah' is an adjective of an omitted 'ikhtiyarah', so the wife's statement is 'I chose myself with one choice'.
The proof is based on qiyas (analogy) and linguistic analysis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? The wife's 'ikhtiyar' is a response to tafwid, which is tamlik. Tamlik gives the wife the power to effect divorce with the husband's intention. Since the husband intended three, the wife's act of choice (even if she says 'one') executes his intention. Linguistically, 'wahidah' describes the mode of choice, not the number of divorces.
The key is that the omitted noun 'ikhtiyarah' is indefinite, so 'wahidah' qualifies it as one act of choice.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) might hold that the wife's statement 'with one' limits to one divorce because the delegation is a grant of ownership and the wife's acceptance determines the number. Hanafis give priority to the husband's intention.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the number of divorces: one vs. three.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah affirms the ruling. The mufta bihi position is three divorces. The reasoning is consistent with the Hanafi principle that in tafwid, the wife is a proxy, and the husband's intention sets the scope.
This is the authoritative view in the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah. The issue: a husband says 'amruk biyadik' (your affair is in your hand) intending three divorces. The wife responds 'akhtartu nafsī bi-wāhidah' (I chose myself with one). The ruling is that three divorces occur. The passage explains two points. First, the word 'ikhtiyār' (choice) is a valid response to 'amr bil-yad' because both 'amr bil-yad' and 'takhyīr' (giving choice) are forms of tamlik (granting ownership of divorce). They are equivalent in force. Second, the wife's phrase 'bi-wāhidah' (with one) functions as an adjective for an omitted word 'ikhtiyārah' (a choice). So the full statement is 'I chose myself with one choice'. In Hanafi law, when the husband intends three divorces and delegates authority, any acceptance by the wife, even if she mentions one, results in three divorces because the delegation was for three. This is analogous to the case where the husband says 'intī mukhtīrah' (you have choice) three times and the wife says 'akhtartu' once – three divorces occur. The reasoning uses qiyas and linguistic analysis.

امر بالید (amr bil-yad): The husband's statement 'your affair is in your hand', a form of delegation (tafwīḍ) of divorce.
واحدہ (wāḥidah): In this context, 'one' as an adjective of an omitted 'ikhtiyārah', meaning one act of choice.

Wife's Response 'Tallaqtu Nafsī' Produces Bā'in Divorce
Ruling with proof for bā'in divorce when wife uses explicit words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife responds to general delegation with explicit words and explain why the divorce is bā'in.

Core Idea
When the wife uses explicit words for one divorce in response to 'amr bil-yad', the divorce is irrevocable (bā'in).

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi divorce law. When a husband delegates the power of divorce to his wife using the general phrase 'amruk biyadik' (your affair is in your hand), and the wife responds with clear, explicit words such as 'I have divorced myself' or 'I choose a divorce,' the ruling is that one bā'in (irrevocable) divorce occurs. The key is understanding that the nature of the delegation determines the effect of the wife's response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation phrase
What did the husband say? Typically 'amruk biyadik' or a similar general delegation of the divorce authority.
General delegation is key – it does not specify number or type.
2
Identify the wife's response
Is the response explicit (ṣarīḥ) like 'tallaqtu nafsī bi-wāḥidah' or 'akhtartu nafsī bi-tatlīqah'?
Explicit words directly indicate divorce.
3
Recognize the ruling
One bā'in divorce occurs. The divorce is valid (sahih) and irrevocable.
The wife's number (one) binds the divorce count.
4
Understand the reasoning
Why is it bā'in? Because the delegation (tafwīḍ) is for bā'in by necessity – the husband gave full authority over her affair, implying the most complete form of divorce.
The adjective 'one' in the response matches the delegation.
5
Note the contrast with intention of three
The text mentions that intention of three divorces can be valid in 'amr bil-yad' because the phrase is general, but this does not change the bā'in nature when the wife responds with one.
This part is truncated but indicates the scope of the delegation.

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back his wife during 'iddah except by a new marriage.
تطلیقہ (tatlīqah): One pronouncement of divorce, considered explicit (ṣarīḥ) but in this context leads to bā'in due to delegation.

Three Divorces When Wife Responds with 'One' to Delegation
Ruling on delegation with intention of three and wife's response of one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband delegates divorce with intention of three and the wife responds 'I choose myself with one', and explain the legal reasoning using the concept of tamlik.

Core Idea
In delegation, the wife's response is interpreted broadly; saying 'one' describes her choice, not the divorce count.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in talaq delegation (amr bil yad). The husband gives his wife the power to divorce herself, intending three divorces. She responds 'I have chosen myself with one.' The Hanafi school rules that three divorces occur. We will unpack the reasoning, which hinges on the nature of tamlik (making the wife owner of her affair) and how the wife's words are construed.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
After the husband says 'amruki bi yadik' intending three divorces and the wife responds 'akhtartu nafsi bi wahidah', three divorces occur. This is the explicit ruling from the text.
2
Identify the dalil
The author uses analogy (qiyas): the wife's 'choice' (ikhtiyar) is a valid response because 'amr bil yad' is a form of tamlik (making her owner) similar to option-giving (takhyir). Therefore, her response is legally effective.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'wahidah' (one) in her response is a description of the deleted noun 'ikhtiyarah' (her choice), not a specification of the number of divorces. So it is as if she said 'I have chosen myself with one choosing', and the husband's intention of three is realized.
The key insight: 'one' modifies the act of choosing, not the divorce count.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may interpret the wife's explicit 'one' as limiting the divorce to one, giving weight to her spoken word over the husband's hidden intention.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on differing views of delegation.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (relied upon) because the nature of tamlik gives effect to the husband's intention; the wife's words are interpreted consistently with that delegation.

Commentary

Key Terms

تملیک (tamlik): Making someone owner of something; here, making the wife owner of the right to divorce.
اختیار (ikhtiyar): Choice; the wife's act of choosing herself (i.e., divorcing herself).

Wife's Response to Delegated Divorce: Ba'in Ruling and Intention of Three
Ruling on ba'in divorce and conditions for valid niyyah of three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife responds with 'tallaqtu nafsi bi wahidatin' or 'akhtartu nafsi bi tatliqatin' and explain why the husband's intention of three is valid only in 'amruki bi yadik'.

Core Idea
Wife's response 'tallaqtu nafsi bi wahidatin' yields one ba'in divorce; intention of three only works with general wording.

When a husband delegates divorce to his wife, the legal consequences depend on precise wording. This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies two critical points: first, what type of divorce occurs when the wife responds with certain phrases, and second, when the husband's hidden intention of three divorces is effective. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the rules of talaq in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's responses and ruling
The passage addresses two possible responses: 'tallaqtu nafsi bi wahidatin' (I divorced myself with one) and 'akhtartu nafsi bi tatliqatin' (I chose myself with one divorce). In both cases, the ruling is one ba'in divorce.
2
Understand the rationale for ba'in
The word 'one' qualifies a deleted infinitive: in the first response it is 'ikhtiyarah' (choice) and in the second 'tatliqah' (divorce). The divorce is ba'in because the husband's delegation (tafwid) is inherently for a ba'in divorce – the wife is given full authority over her affair, and her response carries over the ba'in nature.
Tafwid gives the wife ownership of her affair, so her divorce is irreversible (ba'in).
3
Distinguish validity of intention of three
The husband's intention of three divorces is valid only when he says 'amruki bi yadik' because this phrase can be general or specific. Intending three is an intention of generalization. Conversely, if he says 'ikhtari' (choose), the wording does not admit generality, so the intention of three is ineffective.
This distinction is based on the linguistic capacity of the phrases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if she says 'I have divorced myself with one' or 'I have chosen myself with one divorce', then it is one ba'in, because 'one' is a description of a deleted infinitive which in the first is 'ikhtiyarah' and in the second is 'tatliqah', except that it is ba'in because delegation is only in ba'in of necessity due to her ownership of her affair, and her speech emerged as a response to his, so the description mentioned in the delegation becomes mentioned in the effectuation. And the intention of three is only valid in your saying 'amruki bi yadik' because it admits generality and particularity, and the intention of three is an intention of generalization, contrary to his saying 'ikhtari' because it does not admit generality, and we have verified this previously.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage covers two sub-rulings. First, if the wife responds with 'tallaqtu nafsi bi wahidah' (I divorced myself with one) or 'akhtartu nafsi bi tatliqatin' (I chose myself with one divorce), the result is one ba'in divorce. The reason: the 'one' qualifies a deleted infinitive (ikhtiyarah or tatliqah), and the divorce is ba'in because the husband's delegation (tafwid) is for a ba'in divorce, given that the wife is made owner of her affair. Her response matches his delegation, so the ba'in status is carried over. Second, the intention of three divorces is valid only when the husband says 'amruki bi yadik' because that phrase can be general or specific, and intending three is an intention of generality. In contrast, saying 'ikhtari' (choose) does not admit generality, so intention of three is ineffective there. The author notes this has been explained earlier. The Urdu commentary adds that in the first scenario (from the previous lesson), the wife's response 'akhtartu nafsi bi wahidah' gave three divorces only because the husband intended three and the phrase 'amr bil yad' is general; here the wife's different wording ('tallaqtu' or 'bi tatliqatin') changes the outcome.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): A divorce that separates the couple immediately without possibility of revocation during iddah, unless a new marriage contract is made.
تفویض (tafwid): Delegation; the husband authorizing the wife to divorce herself.

Wife's Response to Delegation: One Ba'in Divorce
Ruling on wife's words in reply to amruk biyadik

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife responds to 'amruki biyadik' with 'talaktu nafsi biwahidah' or 'akhtartu nafsi bitatliqah', and explain why the divorce is ba'in based on the attribute of delegation.

Core Idea
A wife's response to kinayah delegation yields one ba'in divorce, not raj'i.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from Ashraf al-Hidaya on delegated divorce. When a husband uses the allusive phrase 'amruki biyadik' and the wife responds with specific words indicating divorce, the school rules that one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce occurs. We will trace the reasoning behind this ruling, focusing on the attribute of the delegation transferring to the wife's response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's delegation phrase and its legal status.
The husband says 'amruki biyadik', which is kinayah (allusive). Under Hanafi law, kinayah in divorce requires intention and results in ba'in divorce, except three specific words.
The three exceptions are direct words: 'anti taliq', 'ant al-ḥurrah', and 'ant al-bar'ah'.
2
Examine the wife's possible responses.
The wife may say either 'talaktu nafsi biwahidah' (I divorced myself with one) or 'akhtartu nafsi bitatliqah' (I chose myself with a divorcing). In both, 'wahidah' (one) is an adjective for an implied infinitive that matches the verb: for 'talaktu' it is 'tatliq', for 'akhtartu' it is 'ikhtiyar'.
This is based on grammatical analysis of the source.
3
Determine why the divorce is ba'in.
Since the husband's delegation was through kinayah (except the three explicit words), the divorce effected by the wife carries the attribute of the delegation: ba'in. The wife's speech is a response, so the attribute of separation (ba'in) transfers.
The delegation made the wife owner of her affair, which implies ba'in, not raj'i.
4
Distinguish between 'amruki biyadik' and 'ikhtiyari' regarding multiple intentions.
In 'amruki biyadik', the husband can validly intend three divorces because the phrase admits generality (umum). In 'ikhtiyari', it does not, so only one divorce is possible. This is based on earlier discussion in the chapter on ikhtiyar.
This distinction is important for cases where the husband claims he intended three.

His saying: 'Ikhtiyari' because it does not admit generality, and we have verified this before. Book of Divorce. Translation: And if he said, 'I divorced myself with one,' or 'I chose myself with a divorcing,' then it is one ba'in divorce because 'wahidah' is an adjective for an omitted infinitive, and that infinitive in the first case is 'ikhtiyar' and in the second case is 'tatliqah.' But it [the divorce] will be ba'in because the delegation is only in ba'in [divorce] due to the woman being owner of her affair. And the woman's speech emerges as a response to the man's speech. So the attribute mentioned in the delegation will also be mentioned in the occurrence [of divorce]. And in your saying 'amruki biyadik,' intending three is valid because it admits generality and particularity, and intending three is intending division. Unlike his saying 'ikhtiyari' because it does not admit generality, and we have proven this before. Explanation: The issue is that if the woman says in response to 'amruki biyadik': 'I divorced myself with one' or 'I chose myself with a divorcing,' then one ba'in divorce occurs. The proof is that 'wahidah' is an adjective for an omitted infinitive. So the implied infinitive will be assumed according to the preceding verb. Thus in the previous case of 'akhtartu nafsi biwahidah,' 'ikhtiyar' is assumed, and in the second case of 'talaktu nafsi biwahidah,' 'tatliq' is omitted. Now as to why these words cause a ba'in divorce: because 'amruki biyadik' is from among allusive words, and with allusive words ba'in divorce occurs, except for three words that have passed. So it is established that the husband has delegated a ba'in divorce because he made the woman owner of her affair, and making owner entails separation. Summary: The woman is delegated a ba'in divorce, and her speech occurs in response to the man's speech. Hence the attribute of separation mentioned in the delegation will also be mentioned in the effecting of divorce so that the woman's speech matches the man's speech. The author of Hidayah says: In 'amruki biyadik,' intending three divorces is correct, unlike in 'ikhtiyari,' because 'amr bil-yad' admits both generality and particularity. Shaykh al-Islam said that 'amr' is a general noun, including everything. Allah Almighty said: 'And the command on that Day belongs to Allah,' meaning all things. So when he intends divorce by it, it becomes kinayah from 'talaquki biyadik,' and divorce is an infinitive admitting generality and particularity. So intending three is intending division. Unlike 'ikhtiyari' because it does not admit generality. Its detail has passed in the chapter on ikhtiyar. Benefits: Previously it was mentioned that in response to 'ikhtisari' if she says 'akhtartu nafsi bitatliqah,' according to the author's statement, raj'i divorce would occur. This is a slip. One proof is that in the mentioned issue he said if in response to 'amruki biyadik' she says 'akhtartu nafsi bitatliqah,' then ba'in divorce occurs. So from this issue it is known that in the first issue also ba'in divorce occurs because in these two issues there is no difference according to the author. And Allah knows best what is correct.

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya explains the ruling when a husband says 'amruki biyadik' (your affair is in your hand) and the wife responds with either 'talaktu nafsi biwahidah' (I divorced myself with one) or 'akhtartu nafsi bitatliqah' (I chose myself with a divorcing). The commentator clarifies that in both cases one ba'in (irrevocable) divorce occurs. The reasoning is that 'wahidah' (one) is an adjective for an implied infinitive that matches the verb used: for 'akhtartu' it is 'ikhtiyar' (choice), and for 'talaktu' it is 'tatliq' (divorcing). The divorce is ba'in because the husband's delegation through kinayah (allusive) words—except three specific words—gives the wife ownership of her matter, which implies separation. The commentator also addresses the difference between 'amruki biyadik' and 'ikhtiyari': in the former, the husband can validly intend three divorces because the phrase admits generality, while in 'ikhtiyari' it does not. This is based on earlier discussions in the chapter on ikhtiyar. The passage also corrects a previous slip where it was thought that raj'i divorce occurs; the correct view is ba'in in both cases.

Key Terms
کِنَایَة (kinayah): Allusive words that do not explicitly indicate divorce but can imply it when intended.
بَائِن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce that does not allow the husband to take back the wife without a new marriage.
عُمُوم (umum): Generality; the quality of a word that includes all individuals or instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a husband says 'amruki biyadik' and the wife responds 'akhtartu nafsi biwahidah', what type of divorce occurs?
A) One raj'i divorce
B) One ba'in divorce
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
When a wife responds to kinayah delegation by indicating one divorce, the resulting divorce is ba'in, not raj'i.

Delegation for Consecutive Days: Single Authority Rule
Ruling and reasoning for consecutive days in delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a delegation covering consecutive days without a gap is a single authority, and state the consequence of repudiation on the first day.

Core Idea
‘Today and tomorrow’: consecutive times = one delegation; repudiating today ends all.

This lesson examines a specific delegation scenario where the husband grants authority for ‘today and tomorrow.’ Unlike cases with intervening gaps, these days are consecutive, making the delegation indivisible. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings on talaq and khiyar in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its structure
Locate the husband's statement: 'amruki biyadik al-yawm wa ghadan'. Note that 'al-yawm' (today) and 'ghadan' (tomorrow) are directly adjacent – no day of the same kind separates them.
The night between them is of a different kind and is automatically included.
2
Apply the rule of continuity
Since there is no homogeneous time gap (i.e., no full day) between the two mentioned times, the delegation is considered one continuous authority. The night is subsumed.
This contrasts with ‘after tomorrow’ which leaves a gap of a day, creating two separate authorities.
3
Determine the consequence of repudiation
If the wife repudiates the authority on today, the entire delegation ends – she does not retain authority for tomorrow. Because it is a single authority, exercising it or rejecting it on the first day exhausts the delegation.
She cannot wait until tomorrow to use the authority if she rejected it today.
4
Distinguish from cases with gaps
Compare with the scenario where the husband says ‘amruki biyadik al-yawm wa ba‘da ghad’ (today and after tomorrow). There, tomorrow becomes a gap of the same kind, so each day is a separate delegation. Repudiation on today does not affect tomorrow.
The key is whether a full day of the same category intervenes.

Source Text

And if he said: 'Your affair is in your hand today and tomorrow,' the night enters into that. And if she repudiated the authority on her day, the authority does not remain in her hand for tomorrow, because this is one authority, since there is not between the two mentioned times a time of their own kind that his speech does not include, and it [the authority] rushes [i.e., is continuous].

Commentary
This short passage completes the ruling for the case where the husband says 'amruki biyadik al-yawm wa ghadan' (today and tomorrow). Unlike the previous case where there was a gap (after tomorrow), here 'today' and 'tomorrow' are consecutive without a gap of a similar unit (since the night is between day and day, but the night is of a different kind? Actually the text says there is no time of their own kind that is not included; but the night enters because the days are directly adjacent? Wait: The text says 'the night enters' because there is no intervening unit of the same kind (i.e., no day between them). So the delegation is considered one continuous authority covering both today and tomorrow, including the night in between. Therefore, if the wife repudiates on today, she loses the authority for tomorrow as well, because it is a single delegation. This contrasts with the previous case where 'after tomorrow' left a gap (tomorrow itself) that is of the same kind as the days mentioned, so it created two separate authorities. Hence, the ruling depends on whether there is a homogeneous time gap between the mentioned times.

Key Terms
غَد (ghad): Tomorrow; the day following the current day.
ردت (raddat): She repudiated or rejected the authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is transferred to the husband

Key Takeaway
A delegation covering consecutive days without a homogeneous gap is a single authority; repudiation on the first day extinguishes the right, but silence preserves it for the second day.

Amruki bi-Yadiki al-Yawm wa Ba'da Ghad – Two Separate Commands
Explains the ruling for option on non-consecutive days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the phrase 'amruki bi-yadiki al-yawm wa ba'da ghad' and explain why it is two separate commands, distinguishing between talaq and the option (amr bi al-yad).

Core Idea
When the husband specifies two non-consecutive days, the option is two independent grants; revocation of one does not cancel the other.

This lesson examines a specific delegation wording in talaq: the husband gives his wife the option to choose on 'today and the day after tomorrow'. The key legal question is whether this constitutes one continuous option or two separate ones. The Hanafi school, based on the wording and the nature of delegation, rules that it is two separate commands. We will unpack the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and his students, and contrast it with the dissenting view of Imam Zufar, who analogized to talaq. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly applying rulings in cases of delegated divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exact phrase
The husband says: 'amruki bi-yadiki al-yawm wa ba'da ghad' (Your affair is in your hand today and the day after tomorrow). Note the two time periods: 'today' (al-yawm) and 'the day after tomorrow' (ba'da ghad). There is a gap of one full day (tomorrow) between them.
The word 'yawm' (day) is singular, which in Hanafi usul excludes the night.
2
Understand the Hanafi position
According to the Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah), this phrase creates two separate and independent grants of the option. The wife may exercise or revoke each option on its respective day, and revocation on the first day does not affect the second.
This is based on the explicit mention of two distinct times separated by a gap of the same kind (a day).
3
Recognize Zufar's dissenting argument
Imam Zufar argued that the phrase is like 'anti taliq al-yawm wa ba'da ghad' (you are divorced today and the day after tomorrow), where the talaq is one continuous event. He held that the option is one command, so revoking the first day nullifies the entire option.
Zufar's view is a minority opinion within the broader Hanafi tradition.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
The Hanafi scholars rebut Zufar by pointing out a fundamental difference: talaq cannot be split across non-consecutive days – it is instantaneous and continuous. But the option (amr bi al-yad) can be timed. Therefore, the two times are independent, and the second is a separate command.
The ruling treats the phrase as if he said: 'Your affair is in your hand today, and your affair is in your hand the day after tomorrow.'
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under the Hanafi view, if the wife revokes on the first day, the second day's option remains. Under Zufar's view, revocation ends everything. The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is the Hanafi position.
This difference is the thamarah al-khilaf – the practical effect of the legal disagreement.

داخل نہ ہو گی۔ چنانچہ اگر عورت نے اپنے آپ کو رات میں اختیار کر لیا تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ اور اگر عورت نے آج کے دن کا اختیار رد کر دیا تو صرف اسی آج کے دن کا امر بالید رد ہو گا اور کل آئندہ کے بعد میں امر بالید کو رد کر دینے سے بعد غیر کا امر بالید رد نہیں ہوگا۔ پہلے دعویٰ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے چونکہ یوم کولفظ مفرد کے ساتھ ذکر کیا ہے۔ اسلئے یہ اختیارات کو شامل نہیں ہوگا۔ دوسرے دعوی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس شخص نے بصراحت دو وقتوں کو ذکر کیا ہے یعنی یوم اور بعد غد اور ان دونوں کے درمیان کا ہم جنس ایک وقت ایسا ہے۔ جس کو امر بالید شامل نہیں یعنی غد پس یہ مستقلاً دو امر ہو گئے۔ ایک آج کے دن کا امر بالید دوم بعد غد کا۔ اور احد الامریں کو رد کرنے سے آخر رد نہیں ہوتا ۔ لہذا یہاں بھی امر الیوم کو رد کرنے سے بعد غذا کا امر رد نہیں ہو گا۔ امام زفر فر ماتے ہیں کہ دونوں امر ، امر واحد ہیں۔ لہذا اگر آج کے دن کا امر بالید رد کر دیا گیا تو بعد غد کا بھی باقی نہیں رہے گا۔ اور یہ انت طالق اليوم و بعد غد کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ ہماری طرف سے جواب یہ ہے کہ امر بالید کوطلاق پر قیاس کرنا ہی نہیں۔ کیونکہ طلاق تعیین وقت کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی ہے۔ چنانچہ جو عورت یوم میں مطلقہ ہوگی وہ غدا اور بعد غد میں بھی مطلقہ ہوگی اور امر بالید تعیین کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ چنان پر امرک بیدک الیوم و بعد غد میں امر متعین ہے وقت اول یعنی یوم کے ساتھ۔ اور وقت ثانی (بعد غد ) کو مستقلاً دوسرا امر قرار دے دیا جائے گا۔ تقدیری عبارت ہوگی امرک بیدک الیوم و امرک بیدک بعد غد
He said: 'Your affair is in your hand today and the day after tomorrow.' The night is not included because 'yawm' is singular. So there are two commands. Revoking one does not cancel the other. Zufar said they are one command, like saying 'anti taliq al-yawm wa ba'da ghad.' We reply: talaq does not bear timing, while the option does. So the first is timed, and the second is a separate command.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is from Kitab al-Talaq and addresses the ruling when a husband says to his wife: 'Your affair is in your hand today and the day after tomorrow' (amruki bi-yadiki al-yawm wa ba'da ghad). The key issue is whether this constitutes one or two separate grants of the option (amr bi al-yad). The Hanafi position, as stated in the matn of Hidayah, is that it is two separate commands. The reason is that the husband explicitly mentioned two distinct time periods (today and the day after tomorrow) with a gap (tomorrow) in between that is of the same kind (a day) but is not included in the command. Since the word 'yawm' is used in the singular form (mufrad), it does not include the night. Therefore, the two time periods are separate grants. If the wife revokes the option on the first day, the option for the second day remains. Imam Zufar disagreed, analogizing this to the case of talaq where saying 'anti taliq al-yawm wa ba'da ghad' results in a single talaq affecting both times. The Hanafi response is that talaq cannot be specified with a time gap (it is instantaneous and continuous), whereas the option (amr bi al-yad) can be specified with time. Therefore, the first time period is bound with the option, and the second is considered a separate command. The eventual wording is as if he said: 'Your affair is in your hand today, and your affair is in your hand the day after tomorrow.' This is the relied-upon view from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الأمر باليد (al-amr bi al-yad): The option given to the wife to divorce herself or choose her husband by revoking the option.
بعد غد (ba'da ghad): The day after tomorrow, i.e., two days after the present day.
اليوم (al-yawm): The current day, from dawn to sunset (excluding night).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'Amruki bi-yadiki al-yawm wa ba'da ghad.' If the wife revokes the option on the first day, what is the ruling regarding the option for the day after tomorrow?
A) The option for the day after tomorrow is also revoked.
B) The option for the day after tomorrow remains valid and effective.
C) The option is automatically exercised and divorce occurs.
D) The husband must confirm the option again.

Key Takeaway
When a husband specifies 'today and the day after tomorrow', the option is two separate commands; revocation on the first day leaves the second day's option intact.

Talaq — Divorce

Ruling on 'Amruki bi-Yadiki al-Yawm wa Ghadan' – One Command with Night Included
Explains the ruling for option on consecutive days including night

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the option for consecutive days is one continuous command and explain why revocation on the first day ends the entire option according to Zahir al-Riwayah.

Core Idea
Consecutive times make one continuous option; night does not break the counsel session.

In the previous lesson, we saw that if the husband grants the option for today only, the wife may use it within that day. But what if he says 'today and tomorrow'? Does the night between them break the continuity or is it included? This passage resolves that when the two times are consecutive with no gap of a like time, the night is included and the whole is one command. It also examines the effect of the wife revoking the option on the first day and an intra-Hanafi difference on whether the revocation is effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Wording
The husband says 'amruki bi-yadiki al-yawm wa ghadan' (your affair is in your hand today and tomorrow). Note that 'ghadan' is immediately after 'al-yawm' without a separating like time.
2
Determine if One or Two Commands
Because the times are consecutive with no gap of a like time (e.g., no full day in between), the night is included, and the counsel session (majlis al-mashwarah) is not cut off. Thus, it is one continuous command, analogous to saying 'for two days'.
Abu Yusuf distinguishes if the phrase is repeated separately.
3
Analyze the Effect of Revocation
If the wife revokes (radd al-amr) on the first day, she has exercised her choice to keep the marriage. Since she is given a choice between two things (divorce herself or stay married), once she chooses her husband by revoking, the option is exhausted and she cannot choose herself on the second day.
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
4
Consider the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Hanifa (in Nawadir) holds that the wife cannot revoke the option (like talaq), so her revocation is ineffective and she retains the right to choose herself the next day. Abu Yusuf adds a nuance: if the husband says 'amruki bi-yadiki al-yawm wa amruki bi-yadiki ghadan' (separate predicate for each day), it becomes two separate commands.
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi
The text says 'وجه الظاهر' (the manifest view), indicating the Zahir al-Riwayah position is relied upon: revocation on the first day ends the entire option.
The Nawadir narration is noted but not operative.
